City of Portland v. Steven P. Roth


CA No. A66485


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


130 Ore. App. 179; 880 P.2d 967; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 1350


December 6, 1993, Argued and submitted  
September 7, 1994, Decided  
September 7, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Denied January 24, 1995 (320 Or 507).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court, Multnomah County. Thomas L. Moultrie, Judge. No. DA405565-8910.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged his conviction in the District Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) for disobeying a police officer at an assembly, a violation of Portland City Code § 14.24.100. Defendant argued that the ordinance under which he was convicted was preempted by Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.315.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his conviction under city ordinance, alleging that the state statute permitted behavior prohibited by the ordinance. The court reversed defendant's conviction, holding that: 1) the state statute permitted passive resistance, which might manifest itself in the form of refusing to obey a police officer's order to disperse; 2) because the city ordinance prohibited conduct specifically permitted by the state statute, it was preempted by the state statute; 3) preemption was required although there was demonstrably very little overlap between the two pieces of legislation; and 4) the portion of the ordinance that was relevant to defendant's conviction could not be severed from the portion of the ordinance deemed unconstitutional.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court that had been entered on a jury verdict convicting defendant of disobeying a police officer at an assembly.

Lowe v. Keisling


CA No. A84110


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


130 Ore. App. 1; 882 P.2d 91; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 1345


August 18, 1994, Argued and submitted; September 1, 1994, Resubmitted In Banc

September 1, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Rodney W. Miller, Judge. No. 93C-11972.



DISPOSITION: On appeal, reversed and remanded with instructions to allow defendant's motion for summary judgment on counts 2 and 3 and for entry of judgment for defendant; affirmed on cross-appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant secretary of state and intervenor voter appealed an order of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), which granted summary judgment to plaintiff citizens and declared that a proposed initiative seeking to amend the Oregon Constitution to prohibit governments from preventing discrimination against homosexuals violated Ore. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d) and art. XVII, § 2. Plaintiffs cross-appealed the dismissal of alternative claims.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff citizens sought to enjoin a proposed ballot initiative to amend the Oregon Constitution to prohibit governments from preventing discrimination against homosexuals. The court reversed summary judgment for plaintiffs on their claim that the initiative violated Ore. Const. art. IV, § 1(2)(d), holding that the initiative concerned only a single subject. The court also reversed summary judgment on plaintiffs' claim that the initiative violated Ore. Const. art. XVII, § 2, holding that the ballot measure sought an amendment, rather than a revision, of the constitution. While the trial court incorrectly concluded that plaintiffs lacked standing to assert their remaining claims, the court nevertheless affirmed dismissal of their due process and equal protection claims because they were attacks on the constitutionality of the measure if enacted, and therefore were improper pre-enactment challenges to the proposed measure. The court affirmed dismissal of the claim that U.S. Const. art. IV, § 4 prohibited the use of an initiative to enact an amendment that imposed disabilities on a group of citizens because issues under the Guaranty Clause were nonjusticiable political questions.


OUTCOME: The court reversed summary judgment for plaintiff citizens, holding that a proposed ballot initiative concerned only one subject and properly sought to amend, not revise, the constitution. The court affirmed dismissal of plaintiffs' Guaranty Clause claim because it involved nonjusticiable political questions. An injunction prohibiting defendant secretary of state from certifying the initiative was reversed.

 

852 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Page


CA No. A81247


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


129 Ore. App. 558; 879 P.2d 903; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 1200


June 10, 1994, Argued and submitted  
August 10, 1994, Decided  
August 10, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Clatsop County. Philip L. Nelson, Judge. No. 92-6528.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment of the District Court, Clatsop County (Oregon), which convicted him of violating OAR 635-05-200 for possessing or landing pink shrimp from any one trip or landing that exceeded an average count of 160 whole shrimp per pound.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was a commercial shrimper. State police officers saw shrimp being unloaded from his fishing vessel to a seafood plant. Unofficial shrimp samples taken by the plant's employees revealed counts that ranged from 180 to 190 shrimp per pound. Defendant was charged with violating OAR 635-05-200. At trial, the officers testified that each of the shrimp they sampled had a full body but legs, antennae, or other appendages were missing. Defendant filed a motion for a judgment of acquittal, claiming that there was insufficient evidence that the samples tested were whole unbroken shrimp. The trial court denied the motion. A jury convicted defendant. The court held that the plain, natural, and ordinary meaning of the terms "whole shrimp" and "whole unbroken shrimp" in OAR 635-05-200 was shrimp that possessed whole bodies. The evidence introduced was sufficient for the jury to conclude that defendant landed shrimp exceeding an average count of 160 whole shrimp per pound. The language of OAR 635-05-200 was sufficiently definite for persons of common intelligence to understand that the prohibited conduct was landing shrimp whose bodies were too small.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

State v. Kitzman


CA No. A77967


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


129 Ore. App. 520; 879 P.2d 1326; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 1208


January 21, 1994, Argued and submitted  
August 10, 1994, Decided  
August 10, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Kitzman's Petition for Review and State's Petition for Review Allowed December 20, 1994 (320 Or 492). See later issue Oregon Reports.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Lincoln County. Charles P. Littlehales, Judge. No. 922182.



DISPOSITION: Convictions on counts IX through XIV reversed and remanded for a new trial; convictions on counts I through VIII affirmed and remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of conviction in the Circuit Court, Lincoln County (Oregon) of rape, sexual abuse, and unlawful sexual penetration involving young children under the age of 12 years.


OVERVIEW: The eldest child testified at trial, and the statements of the two younger children were admitted under the hearsay exception in Or. Evid. Code 803(18a)(b). The court held that the trial court's interpretation of 803(18a)(b), which gave defendant no opportunity to confront the younger children to determine if they were, in fact, competent to testify or that they were not credible, violated defendant's constitutional right of confrontation. According, the court reversal the convictions involving these victims. The convictions relating to the eldest child were not inextricably intertwined with the other children's statements. It was not error to deny defendant's motion to compel election as the jury instruction sufficiently ensured that the jury convicted only upon a determination that the same act had occurred on one occasion. There was no abuse of discretion in denying defendant's motion to sever the offenses as the evidence was not so confusing that the jury was unable to consider the offenses separately. Violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 418.747(3), (4) was not a ground for dismissing an indictment, suppressing evidence, or granting a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions as to charges of rape and sexual abuse of the two younger victims and remanded the charges for a new trial. The remaining convictions were affirmed and remanded for resentencing.

Stranahan v. Fred Meyer, Inc.


CA No. A75224


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON

July 27, 1994, Filed

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Lee Johnson, Judge. No. 9110-06504.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff activist sought review of an order from the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which granted defendant retailer's motion for summary judgment in the activist's false arrest and malicious prosecution suit.


OVERVIEW: The activist was arrested for trespass while attempting to gather initiative petition signatures on the premises of one of the retailer's stores. The criminal proceedings were terminated without a conviction, so the activist filed a false arrest and malicious prosecution suit against the retailer. The trial court granted the retailer's motion for summary judgment, but on appeal, the court held that there were fact issues that precluded summary judgment for the retailer. The court further held that the retailer's reliance on favorable but ultimately reversed court decisions was no defense to the false arrest claim because the appellate level decisions on the issue established that the activist had not unlawfully been on the premises. The court also held that the retailer' reliance on the ultimately reversed cases did not provide a defense against the malicious prosecution suit because the evidence showed that the prosecution may have been motivated by malice against the activist. Finally, the court held the activist was not precluded from recovering punitive damages.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded an order from the trial court, which granted the retailer's motion for summary judgment.

State ex rel. Juvenile Dep't v. Orozco (In re Orozco)

The juvenile court also ordered defendant to provide a blood sample for DNA testing pursuant to former Or. Rev. Stat. § 419507(11)(a) and Or. Rev. Stat. § 137.076. Defendant appealed.

856 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


City of Eugene v. Kruk


CA No. A71272


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


128 Ore. App. 415; 875 P.2d 1190; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 909


June 23, 1993, Submitted on remand from the Oregon Supreme Court  
June 8, 1994, Decided  
June 8, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On remand from the Oregon Supreme Court. City of Eugene v. Kruk, 316 Or 436, 851 P2d 1140 (1993). Appeal from District Court, Lane County. Bryan T. Hodges, Judge. (Trial). Frank R. Alderson, Judge. (Demurrer). No. 91-50006.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a case on remand from the Oregon Supreme Court, defendant appealed her conviction for conduct that interfered with a police officer's lawful performance of a duty, a violation of Eugene, Or., Municipal Code § 4.907. The trial court overruled defendant's demurrer that alleged that § 4.907 was unconstitutional on its face under Or. Const. art. XI, § 2, which prohibited localities from enacting laws that conflicted with state criminal laws.


OVERVIEW: On remand, the court considered whether § 4.907 was in conflict with Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 162.235 and § 162.315, which dealt with interference with the administration of any governmental or judicial function and resistance to arrest. The court applied a three-part test: it examined the ordinance and statutes, it determined what conduct the ordinance prohibited, and it looked to see whether the applicable statutes permitted that conduct, either expressly, by its legislative history, or otherwise. The court found that § 162.235 applied to conduct other than resisting arrest and required that the prohibited conduct be manifested by threats, violence, or physical interference, a limitation that recognized certain constitutional safeguards such as freedom of speech and assembly. The court further found that § 162.315 expressly excluded passive resistance from the crime of resisting arrest. The court held that these statutes allowed a passive refusal to leave a particular area in response to a lawful order from a police officer, an act that Eugene, Or., Municipal Code § 4.907 prohibited. The court held the ordinance therefore violated Or. Const. art. XI, § 2.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction under a city ordinance for conduct that interfered with a police officer's lawful performance of a duty.

Leckie & Voorhies


CA No. A79785


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


128 Ore. App. 289; 875 P.2d 521; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 775


April 21, 1994, Argued and submitted  
May 25, 1994, Decided  
May 25, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Ann Aiken, Judge. No. 60-92-06326.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sperm donor appealed from the order of the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), which entered summary judgment against his filiation claim filed against respondent recipient under Or. Rev. Stat. § 109.125.


OVERVIEW: The sperm donor entered into an artificial insemination agreement with the recipient and her partner. After the parties' daughter was born, the sperm donor was permitted to visit his daughter on a regular basis, but when the daughter was three years old, his visitation became more limited. Thereafter, the sperm donor filed a filiation claim against the recipient, and the trial court entered summary judgment against the sperm donor. On appeal, the court affirmed. The court held that by signing the agreement, which the parties reaffirmed when the daughter was three years old, the sperm donor expressly waived his right to assert any parental rights, including the prosecution of a filiation claim under § 109.125. The court held that the parties' conduct after the daughter's birth was not legally sufficient to vitiate his waiver and, further, that the sperm donor failed to present any evidence of the parties' conduct after they reaffirmed the agreement.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order entering summary judgment in favor of the recipient and against the sperm donor's filiation claim.

Henderson v. Department of Agric.


CA No. A76943


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


128 Ore. App. 169; 875 P.2d 487; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 786


September 27, 1993, Argued and submitted  
May 25, 1994, Decided  
May 25, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review from Department of Agriculture. No. 603-714-NR021-91.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, pesticide applicator and his employer, sought judicial review of an order of respondent Oregon Department of Agriculture (ODA), which imposed civil penalties for four violations of the Pesticide Control Act, Or. Rev. Stat. Ch. 634.


OVERVIEW: The ODA concluded that the pesticide applicator and his employer administered pesticides in an inconsistent manner with the directions on their labels in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 634.372(2). The pesticide applicator and his employer claimed that the ODA misinterpreted key terms in the statute. The court affirmed the ODA's decision and held that § 634.372(2) did not violate the state constitutional prohibition against delegating legislative authority to private entities because the manufacturers' labels were subject to specific requirements imposed by a comprehensive federal regulatory scheme. The ODA's definition of the word "expose" was consistent with a commentary drafted by the Environmental Protection Agency, which recognized that the word was subject to broad interpretation. The ODA's construction of the statute was also consistent with the legislature's intent of regulating the use of pesticides in the public interest, and it preserved the statutory distinctions between intentional and negligent misapplications. Alleged impropriety in imposing civil penalties was not grounds for dismissing the violations.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the order that found that the pesticide applicator and his employer violated the Pesticide Control Act.

OVERVIEW: The former employer argued that the trial court erred in denying its motions to withdraw from the jury the issue of its vicarious liability for conduct of a sheriff that issued the criminal citation for theft in the second degree against the former employee and that Or. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a decision of the Circuit Court, Lake County (Oregon), which entered a pretrial order pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 138.060(3) that suppressed evidence discovered in defendant's home in proceedings wherein defendant was charged with first-degree manslaughter under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.118 and felon in possession of a firearm under Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.610.


OVERVIEW: Medical examiners found an alleged suicide victim in defendant's house and talked with defendant in his backyard. Sheriffs searched defendant's home pursuant to a warrant issued by a judge from another county. Defendant was charged with first-degree manslaughter under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.118 and felon in possession of a firearm under Or. Rev. Stat. § 161.610. The trial court suppressed evidence pursuant to Or. Rev. The court ruled further that the search of defendant's home was not illegal although the executing officer had not read the warrant to him as required by Or. Rev. Stat. § 133.575 because there was no aggravated violation of the statute or of defendant's constitutional rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's ruling that had entered an pretrial order suppressing evidence, and remanded the matter for further proceedings.

State v. Cookman


CA No. A73459


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


127 Ore. App. 283; 873 P.2d 335; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 611


January 25, 1993, Argued and submitted; December 8, 1993, Resubmitted In Banc  
April 20, 1994, Decided  
April 20, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Allowed August 9, 1994 (319 Or 572).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Charles A. Sams, Judge. No. 91-1524.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State sought review, en banc, of the court's affirmation of the trial court's order allowing defendant's demurrer to an indictment charging him with sexual misconduct in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 163.670,163.673, 163.425 on the grounds that the State failed to bring the charges within the applicable statute of limitations found that Or. Rev. Stat. § 131.125(2).


OVERVIEW: Defendant allegedly engaged in certain acts of sexual misconduct in 1986. Prior to its amendment in 1991, § 131.125(2) provided that the statute of limitations for defendant's alleged misconduct was three years. Under § 131.125(2) as it read at the time of his alleged violations, the statute of limitations ran in 1989. In amending § 131.125(2), the Oregon Legislature extended the statute of limitations to six years and gave it retroactive effect. The State attempted to prosecute defendant. Defendant argued that to apply § 131.125(2) retroactively to resurrect a criminal action against him when the statutory period had already run violated his U.S. Const. amend. 14 due process rights, violated fundamental fairness, and was an ex post facto law. Concentrating on the issue of fundamental fairness, the court held that § 131.125(2) was invalid insofar as it purported to revive criminal prosecutions that were previously time-barred. The court ruled that the three-year statute of limitations applied to defendant and the State was barred from commencing an action against defendant.


OUTCOME: The en banc court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

City of Portland v. Lot 11, Block 55, Irvington

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a civil forfeiture action, claimant appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), entered in favor of plaintiff city.


OVERVIEW: City police officers executed a search warrant and seizure order on claimant's residence. The officers found in the basement of the residence 31 adult marijuana plants, a number of "starter" plants, six baggies of marijuana, $ 3,020 in cash, plastic bag drug packaging materials, a scale, marijuana seeds, and grow equipment, which included transformers, ballasts and halide light fixtures. The court found ample evidence in the record to support the trial court's finding that the marijuana that the city police officers seized was not produced for personal use.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state sought review of a denial by the Circuit Court, Douglas County (Oregon), of the state's request to revoke the outpatient status of appellee committed individual.

866 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


AG W. Supply v. Hall


CA No. A77538


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


126 Ore. App. 475; 869 P.2d 383; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 248


September 24, 1993, Argued and submitted  
February 23, 1994, Decided  
February 23, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Paul J. Lipscomb, Judge. No. 92C-10301.



DISPOSITION: Reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant, the state fire marshal, challenged an order of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon) that declared that Or. Rev. Stat. § 480.360 was unconstitutional and enjoined enforcement of that statute and other provisions related to it against plaintiff agricultural cooperatives and corporations. The statute governed the dispensing of gasoline.


OVERVIEW: The agricultural cooperatives provided cardlock gasoline dispensing services to their members and persons who purchased gasoline through cardlock facilities. The gasoline dispensing laws required that customers purchase a minimum of 2,400 gallons of fuel per year and set certain exemptions. The trial court found that Or. Rev. Stat. § 480.360 violated the Or. Const. art. I, §20 and the Equal Protection Clause. On appeal, the court reversed. It found that the gallonage exemption did not violate the Or. Const. art. I, § 20, which guaranteed unjustified denial of equal privileges or immunities to individual citizens as well as unjustified differentiation among classes of citizens. The statutory exemption did not make a distinction that fit the definition of a true class. The classification it created was based on characteristics that the law defined and was not one based on characteristics that existed apart from the law. It was not a class cognizable under Or. Const. art. I, § 20. Since there was no true class, there was no constitutional violation. The statute also did not violate the Equal Protection Clause because it was rationally related to a legitimate state interest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment.

City Univ. v. Office of Educ. Policy & Planning


CA No. A70734


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON

February 23, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Allowed May 31, 1994 (319 Or 149).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Albin W. Norblad, Judge. No. 88C-11645.



DISPOSITION: Judgment modified to declare ORS 348.835(2)(c) unconstitutional in its entirety; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, State, educational agency, and associated individuals, sought review of a judgment from the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), which entered judgment in favor of respondent private university in a declaratory judgment action, held that Or. Rev. Stat. § 348.835(2)(c) was unconstitutional, and struck the word "Oregon" from the statute in order to preserve it.


OVERVIEW: The university filed a declaratory judgment action against appellants seeking a declaration that § 348.835(2)(c) was unconstitutional on the ground that the statute required them to comply with accreditation requirements that were not imposed on Oregon schools. The trial court entered judgment in favor of the university and appellants sought review. Appellants challenged the declaratory judgment that held § 348.835(2)(c) was unconstitutional under the Commerce Clause, and struck the word "Oregon" from the statute in order to preserve it. The court determined that § 348.835(2)(c) discriminated against interstate commerce because appellants failed to carry their burden to show that the statute served a legitimate local purpose that could not be served by non-discriminatory means. The court concluded that the trial court erred in severing the word "Oregon" from the statute because the word was inseparably related to the remaining language of the statute. The court modified the trial court judgment and invalidated § 348.835(2)(c) in its entirety.


OUTCOME: The court modified the trial court judgment and declared § 348.835(2)(c) unconstitutional in its entirety.

 

868 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Nelson v. City of Lake Oswego


CA Nos. A78257, A76358


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


126 Ore. App. 416; 869 P.2d 350; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 262


August 13, 1993, Submitted on record and briefs; December 8, 1993, Resubmitted In Banc  
February 23, 1994, Decided  
February 23, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. John Lowe, Judge. (CA A78257). Sid Brockley, Judge. (CA A76358). No. CCV91-12-316. (Cases Consolidated for Opinion Only).



DISPOSITION: Affirmed on both appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff landowners appealed the decision of the Circuit Court, Clackamas County (Oregon), which dismissed the landowners' claims involving nonremonstrance agreements made with defendant city. The dismissal was made because the circuit court found that jurisdiction was with the Land Use Board of Appeals (board). The city appealed the denial of its motion to dismiss the taking claims made by the landowners.


OVERVIEW: When the landowners sought a permit from the city to construct a house, the city required that the landowners obtain a lot line adjustment. That adjustment was allowed, but the landowners had to execute nonremonstrance agreements and pledge that they would not oppose future street improvements. The landowners also had to convey a drainage easement. The landowners did so. Then they complained that the nonremonstrance requirement violated the state and federal constitutions, and that the easement was a taking for which they were entitled to monetary compensation. On appeal, the court agreed that the board had jurisdiction over the nonremonstrance agreements because the claims arose out of a land use decision. The court found, however, that the taking claims were not within the board's exclusive jurisdiction. Therefore, the circuit court's denial of the city's motion to dismiss that claim was correct. The court found that the exhaustion and ripeness doctrines were inapplicable to the inverse condemnation claims because there was nothing left to happen at the local or administrative level. What mattered was that there was a reasonable relationship between the condition and the use.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed both the landowners' appeal of the dismissal of their claims regarding the nonremonstrance agreements as well as the city's appeal of the denial of its motion to dismiss the taking claims filed by the landowners.

 

869 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Nevel


CA No. A74105


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


126 Ore. App. 270; 868 P.2d 1338; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 164


May 10, 1993, Argued and submitted  
February 9, 1994, Decided  
February 9, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Janice R. Wilson, Judge. No. C91-09-34444.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a judgment of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted him of possession, manufacture, and delivery of a controlled substance in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.992(1)(a), (4)(b). He challenged the trial court's denial of his motion to suppress statements obtained after he was stopped and evidence was seized from his person and vehicle.


OVERVIEW: A police officer believed that defendant may have assaulted his wife. While observing defendant, the officer concluded that defendant was under the influence of drugs. The officer stopped defendant and searched his vehicle, which uncovered drugs. Defendant claimed that he was illegally stopped under Or. Rev. Stat. § 131.615(1) and Or. Const. art. I, § 9 when placed in the back of a patrol car. On review, the court upheld defendant's conviction. The stop was lawful because the officer had a reasonable suspicion of a crime. The discovery of drug's in defendant's van was not unrelated to the suspected offense and was permissible under Or. Rev. Stat. § 131.615(3). The court found that the investigatory detention of defendant was as unobtrusive as possible and that a Miranda warning was not required. The court held that defendant was not in custody for purposes of the federal constitution, so his statements need not be excluded from the evidence. There was no basis for suppression of evidence seized from defendant or his van, because the warrantless search was constitutional. Finally, the court concluded that the evidence was sufficient for conviction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.

 

870 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Beugli


CA No. A75452


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


126 Ore. App. 290; 868 P.2d 766; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 166


September 3, 1993, Argued and submitted  
February 9, 1994, Decided  
February 9, 1994, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Marion County. William M. Horner, Judge. No. 91D-103499.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of an order of the District Court, Marion County (Oregon), which dismissed an information charging defendant with six counts of sexual abuse in the second degree, Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.425, four counts of official misconduct in the first degree, § 162.415, and four counts of harassment, § 166.065.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was an Oregon State Police (OSP) officer. As a result of OSP internal investigations, the state filed an information charging defendant with six counts of sexual abuse in the second degree, four counts of official misconduct in the first degree, and four counts of harassment. Defendant successfully moved to dismiss the information, asserting that, because he was compelled to incriminate himself as part of the internal investigation, he was entitled to transactional immunity. On appeal, the court reversed the decision and remanded it to the trial court. The court held that the legislature did not grant defendant transactional immunity as a substitute for his right against self-incrimination and that OSP neither expressly nor impliedly promised that defendant would receive transactional immunity. The court stated that language of the collective bargaining agreement, under which defendant was compelled to cooperate in the investigation, did not promise transactional immunity. Because the state conceded that it violated defendant's right against self-incrimination, the court held that defendant could seek only the usual remedy against unconstitutionally compelled testimony.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the district court and remanded the case so that defendant could seek the usual remedy against unconstitutionally compelled testimony, the suppression of his statements and evidence derived from them.

State v. Macy


CA No. A66654


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


125 Ore. App. 649; 866 P.2d 509; 1994 Ore. App. LEXIS 9


October 29, 1993, Submitted on remand from the Oregon Supreme Court  
January 5, 1994, Decided  
January 5, 1994, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Allowed June 14, 1994 (319 Or 211).


PRIOR HISTORY: On remand from the Oregon Supreme Court. 316 Or 335, 851 P2d 579 (1993). Appeal from Circuit Court, Polk County. H. W. Devlin, Judge. No. 27992.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: On remand from the Oregon Supreme Court was defendant's appeal in respect to consecutive life sentences imposed by the trial court for his murder conviction. Defendant had been found guilty on two counts of murder under former Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115(5).


OVERVIEW: At trial, defendant received concurrent life sentences with a 25-year mandatory minimum. Thereafter, the Oregon Supreme Court held that the 25-year minimum, under § 163.115(5), constituted a disproportionate sentence. Defendant appealed, and the court vacated his sentence and remanded. On remand, the trial court sentenced defendant to consecutive life terms and imposed a 10-year minimum sentence. Defendant appealed the minimum sentence. Subsequently, defendant petitioned for post-conviction relief on the ground that his attorney on the second appeal failed to assign error to the imposition of consecutive sentences. The post-conviction court found that the consecutive sentences were unlawful and granted a delayed appeal. The court held that it lacked authority to do anything except affirm. The Oregon Supreme Court remanded the case on the ground that the delayed appeal was proper. The court affirmed. The court stated that it could not conclude that the second sentence was more severe than the original one because it would not speculate about how a parole board might treat defendant's sentences and his actual length of imprisonment or whether he would ever be paroled.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's consecutive life sentences on the murder conviction.

Goodwin v. State


CA No. A66807


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


125 Ore. App. 359; 866 P.2d 466; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 2132



December 22, 1993, Decided  
December 22, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied April 13, 1994. Petition for Review Denied May 10, 1994 (319 Or 80).


PRIOR HISTORY: On appellant's motion for reconsideration filed October 7, 1993, reconsideration allowed; opinion (116 Or App 279, 840 P2d 1372 (1992); Appeal from Circuit Court, Grant County. R. T. Gooding, Judge. No. 90-05-162 CV.



DISPOSITION: Reconsideration allowed; opinion withdrawn; reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The State moved for reconsideration of the court's opinion in which the court affirmed the judgment of the Circuit Court, Grant County (Oregon) granting petitioner's motion for post-conviction relief. The trial court had agreed with the petitioner that his conviction should be set aside because it was obtained in violation of Or. Const. art. VII (amended), § 5(2), because the grand jury that indicted him consisted of only six grand jurors.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner was convicted of criminal nonsupport and did not appeal. He filed a petition for post-conviction relief, alleging that the indictment that resulted in his conviction was obtained in violation of Or. Const. art. VII (amended), § 5(2). The court initially held that petitioner was entitled to raise the issue during post conviction relief even though it was not raised at trial. An issue was ordinarily reviewable on direct appeal only if it was preserved at trial, but an issue that could have been raised at trial was subject to being raised on post-conviction only if it was not preserved at trial.In reversing the judgment of the trial court, the court held that even if the issuance of the indictment by a grand jury of fewer than seven members was a constitutional violation, that violation did not make the conviction void. Post-conviction relief should be granted when a petitioner is denied a substantial constitutional right that rendered the conviction void, or the court lacks jurisdiction to impose the judgment. A conviction based on a defective indictment, even assuming that the defect was one of constitutional dimensions, was at most voidable, but was not void ab initio.


OUTCOME: The court granted the State's motion for rehearing. It held that petitioner's conviction was not void and reversed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Johansen (In re Johansen)


CA No. A69256


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


125 Ore. App. 365; 866 P.2d 470; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 2133


October 16, 1992, Argued and submitted  
December 22, 1993, Decided  
December 22, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied February 16, 1994. Petition for Review Pending 1994.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Charles Sams, Judge. No. M87-4-11.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a mentally ill patient, challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court, Clackamas County (Oregon), which involuntarily committed her to a state mental hospital for an additional 180 days. The patient had been involuntarily committed for 180 days pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. 426.130(3) on two previous occasions. The patient attacked the constitutionality of the recommitment procedure under Or. Rev. Stat. §§ 426.301-426.307.


OVERVIEW: The patient alleged that the entire recommitment procedure of §§ 426.301-426.307 was unconstitutional on its face under the Due Process Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and under the Or. Const. art. III, § 1, and art. VII, § 1 (amended). She asserted that for the recommitment scheme to be constitutionally adequate, it had to require that an adversarial hearing be provided to every individual against whom a certification for continued commitment was filed. The court held that the state had a substantial interest in allocating priority to the diagnosis and treatment of patients, rather than to time consuming procedural minuets performed after admittance to justify continuing a patient's confinement. The court also held that requiring a formal hearing for every patient in connection with a certification decision would increase the amount of time that mental health specialists would spend preparing for and participating in hearings and would reduce the amount of time they would spend performing the rehabilitative tasks for which they have been trained.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

State v. Bockorny


CA Nos. A70018 (Control), A70019 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


124 Ore. App. 585; 863 P.2d 1296; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1895


June 3, 1993, Argued and submitted  
November 17, 1993, Decided  
November 17, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied January 19, 1994. Petition for Review Denied February 8, 1994 (318 Or 351).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Robert P. Jones, Judge. Nos. C89-10-35769, C90-08-34880.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted her of five counts of aggravated murder under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.115 and one count of murder under Or. Rev. Stat. § 163.095, after she and her husband were tried separately, and sentenced her to life without the possibility of parole.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of aggravated murder and murder after she and her husband were tried separately, and defendant was sentenced to life without the possibility of parole. On appeal, the court affirmed because (1) her husband's violent relationship with a former and his prior bad acts were not relevant or essential under Or. Evid. Code 404(3) to showing that, at the time defendant committed her criminal acts, she was compelled to do so; (2) it was true that the testimony of the state's witness tended to show that defendant was not truthful, but it was not error to admit the testimony as the witness made no direct comments about defendant's credibility; (3) the state's closing argument did not deny defendant a fair trial because the state was not making a definitive statement about the jury's verdict in her husband's case; and (4) defendant's constitutional challenge to her "true life" sentence was first raised in her motion in arrest of judgment, so that it was not preserved for review because the attack on the validity of her sentence had nothing to do with the legal authority of the grand jury that indicted her or the language of the charges set forth in the indictment.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court, which convicted defendant of five counts of aggravated murder and one count of murder, after she and her husband were tried separately, and sentenced her to life without the possibility of parole.

Neher v. Chartier


CA No. A75617


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


124 Ore. App. 220; 862 P.2d 1307; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1844


April 7, 1993, Argued and submitted  
November 3, 1993, Decided  
November 3, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied January 26, 1994. Petition for Review Allowed March 22, 1994 (318 Or 478).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Robert W. Redding, Judge. No. A9112-08001.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff father sought review of an order of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which dismissed his wrongful death action against defendants, a municipal corporation and its employee. The trial court had granted defendants motion for judgment on the pleadings, pursuant to Or. R. Civ. P. 21B, on the grounds that they were immune from liability under Or. Rev. Stat. 30.265(3)(a) for claims covered by workers' compensation.


OVERVIEW: The father was personal representative of his daughter's estate. She was killed when she was crushed beneath a bus while crossing a street in the course of her employment. On appeal, the court affirmed because the trial court did not err in dismissing the claim. The court rejected the father's contention that § 30.265(3)(a) violated Or. Const. art. I, § 10 because the only payment provided under the workers' compensation statute, Or. Rev. Stat. § 656.204(1) was a payment for burial expenses. The court held that a legislative alteration of a cause of action was permissible, as long as the injured party was not left entirely without a remedy and the workers' compensation statute provided a remedy that the court could not say was not substantial enough to satisfy the constitution's requirements. The court rejected the father's equal protection challenges to the statute as well and found there was no constitutional violation based on the granting of the privilege to bring a claim only to those persons injured by tortfeasors other than officers, employees or agents of public bodies.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's dismissal of the father's wrongful death claim against the municipal corporation and its employee.

COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON

PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Kip W. Leonard, Judge. No. 10-90-07017C.



DISPOSITION: Remanded for resentencing on Count II; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from a decision of the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), which imposed sentence on his convictions for two counts of burglary in the first degree for burglarizing two houses. The trial court also denied defendant's motion to exclude his juvenile adjudications from the calculation of his criminal history score.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was charged with two counts of burglary. The first count contained an allegation, enhancing the seriousness of the crime, that defendant burglarized an occupied dwelling. After the trial court rejected defendant's request for an instruction on the mental state required for the penalty enhancer, defendant was convicted on both counts. The trial court sentenced defendant to prison and post-prison supervision on the first count, and 180 days of jail incarceration, consecutive to the prison term, and a term of probation on the second count. On appeal, the court held that: (1) the trial court properly refused defendant's requested instruction because it misstated the law: (2) the trial court properly used defendant's juvenile adjudications to calculate his criminal history score; and (3) the trial court improperly imposed a consecutive sentence because the imposition of a jail term consecutive to a prison term violated Or. Admin. R. 253-12-020(d), the trial court could not impose a sentence of 180 days -- the maximum number of custody units, and the probationary term imposed on the second charge should have merged with the post-prison supervision ordered for the first charge.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's sentence on the first count of burglary, which alleged that defendant burglarized an occupied dwelling, and remanded for resentencing on the second count of burglary.

State v. Cavota


CA No. A73633


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


121 Ore. App. 598; 856 P.2d 322; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1159


May 28, 1993, Argued and submitted  
July 14, 1993, Decided  
July 14, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Marion County. Albin W. Norblad, Judge. No. 91C-20792.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Circuit Court, Marion County (Oregon), which imposed a sentence of life imprisonment with a minimum term of 18 years following his conviction for murder. Defendant had entered a "statutory plea" under ORS 135.407.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, defendant argued that the sentencing guidelines superseded the provisions of ORS 163.115(3), so that the trial court was without authority under the guidelines to impose a sentence of life imprisonment with a minimum term of 18 years. In affirming, the court noted that the trial court had advised defendant of two specific consequences of the agreement. It explained that by agreement of the parties, the only latitude that the trial court retained in its sentencing decision was whether it would impose a 15-year or 18-year minimum term to accompany the mandatory life sentence, depending on defendant's cooperation with the police. The trial court also advised defendant that if it decided to impose a sentence outside of the 15-to 18-year range, defendant could withdraw his plea and face trial on both charges. The trial court then found that defendant had not cooperated with the police and imposed an 18-year minimum sentence consistent with the agreed range. Finally, under ORS 138.222(2)(d), a court could not review any sentence resulting from an agreement. Consequently, the court was precluded from reviewing defendant's claim.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

State v. Plourd


CA No. A73887


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


121 Ore. App. 557; 855 P.2d 1138; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1120


April 28, 1993, Argued and submitted  
July 7, 1993, Decided  
July 7, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Respondent's Motion for Reconsideration Filed July 7, Allowed by Opinion December 8, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Kip W. Leonard, Judge. No. 10-91-03041.



DISPOSITION: Convictions affirmed; remanded for resentencing on Count 3; otherwise affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After his conviction of one count of endangering the welfare of a minor, two counts of sexual abuse in the second degree, and one count of attempted sodomy in the second degree, defendant was sentenced by the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon). Defendant argued that a sentence of consecutive one-year terms on the convictions for sexual abuse was error under Oregon Laws 1989, chapter 790, § 51, as amended by Oregon Laws 1991, chapter 830, § 9.


OVERVIEW: Oregon Laws 1989, chapter 790, § 51, as amended by Oregon Laws 1991, chapter 830, § 9, provided that the maximum term of incarceration for a Class A misdemeanor could not exceed six months unless the trial judge expressly found substantial and compelling reasons to impose a longer term. The statute further provided that the limitations did not apply to certain enumerated offenses. On appeal, the court held that (1) its review of defendant's sentences was under ORS 138.040 and was limited to whether they exceeded the legal maximum or were cruel and unusual; (2) the six-month limitation argued by defendant did not apply to violations of ORS 163.415 and, although the sexual abuse statutes were recodified by Oregon Laws 1991, chapter 830, that recodification did not affect defendant's sentences because they were imposed under the statutes in force when the acts were committed; and (3) under ORS 161.615(1), the maximum term of imprisonment for each sexual abuse conviction was one year. The court accepted the State's concession that the trial court erred in enhancing defendant's criminal history score on the sodomy count by using convictions sentenced at the same proceeding.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions. The sentences were affirmed, except the court remanded for resentencing on the count of attempted sodomy in the second degree.

City of Lake Oswego v. $ 23,232.23 in Cash


CA No. A77119


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


121 Ore. App. 432; 855 P.2d 201; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1119



June 30, 1993, Decided  
June 30, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] On appellant's Motion for Reconsideration filed November 19, 1992; Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Charles A. Sams, Judge. No. 91-5-372.



DISPOSITION: Reconsideration allowed; order adhered to, with leave to renew motion for transcript at state expense.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant claimant sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court, Clackamas County (Oregon), which ordered forfeiture of cash seized from the claimant at the time of his arrest. The indigent claimant filed a motion for reconsideration of the court's order denying his motion to have a transcript prepared at state expense.


OVERVIEW: In response to the claimant's motion for reconsideration, the court asked the State Court Administrator whether the legislature had appropriated funds to pay for transcripts in civil cases. Based on the response, the court found that although no amount within a "mandated payments" fund of the Judicial Department had been specifically earmarked for the payment of transcript fees, the establishment of that fund constituted an appropriation for that purpose. However, the court held that, in order to obtain a transcript at state expense under Or. Rev. Stat. § 21.605(3)(a), an indigent appellant was required to make a prima facie showing that the portion of the transcript necessary to prosecute the appeal would reveal reversible error in the proceedings below. The claimant had not made such a showing. He also failed to show that the fee requirement unconstitutionally discriminated against a true class, as persons who could not afford the transcript fee did not constitute a class. The use of the discretionary term "may" in Or. Rev. Stat. § 21.605(3)(a) indicated that the legislature did not intend to establish a right to a free appeal for every indigent civil litigant.


OUTCOME: The court allowed reconsideration but adhered to its order denying the claimant's motion to have a transcript prepared at state expense.

 

883 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Palmer v. State


CA No. A71861


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


121 Ore. App. 377; 854 P.2d 955; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1037


June 5, 1992, Argued and submitted  
June 23, 1993, Decided  
June 23, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied November 10, Petition for Review Allowed November 30, 1993 (318 Or 97).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Lane County. Maurice K. Merten, Judge. No. 16-90-06124.



DISPOSITION: Judgment on first claim reversed; judgment on second claim affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner appealed the decision of the Circuit Court, Lane County (Oregon), which dismissed his petition for post-conviction relief on the ground that his petition did not state a claim for relief following his conviction for unlawful possession of a firearm in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 166.250.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner filed an application for post-conviction relief. He alleged that his trial counsel was inadequate and that § 166.250 was unconstitutional. On appeal, the court found that petitioner could have raised the statute of limitations as an affirmative defense in an answer or in a motion to dismiss, and that since he did neither, he had waived that defense. In connection with his claim of adequacy of trial counsel, petitioner was required to show, by a preponderance of the evidence, facts demonstrating that trial counsel failed to exercise reasonable professional skill and judgment and that petitioner suffered prejudice as a result. The trial court erred because petitioner was entitled to a hearing on the merits as to whether his counsel failed to argue that the State failed to prove that the gun was concealed on petitioner's person. Further, no statute required petitioner to establish that trial or appellate counsel were inadequate in order to raise a constitutional challenge to the criminal statute for the first time in post-conviction proceedings. The absence of a statutory definition for the term "concealed" in § 166.250 did not render that statute void for vagueness.


OUTCOME: The court reversed that part of the judgment dismissing petitioner's claim that his trial counsel was inadequate. However, the court affirmed that part of the judgment that held that the statute under which petitioner was convicted was not void for vagueness.

 

884 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Gyenes


CA No. A70620


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


121 Ore. App. 208; 855 P.2d 642; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1045


September 28, 1992, Argued and submitted; May 5, 1993, Resubmitted In Banc  
June 23, 1993, Decided  
June 23, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Yamhill County. John W. Hitchcock, Judge. No. 91-085.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state sought review of a judgment from the Circuit Court of Yamhill County (Oregon) that allowed defendant's demurrer and dismissed three counts of bribery under Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.015.


OVERVIEW: Defendant and the state read § 162.051 as meaning that unreported campaign contributions were bribes. Defendant argued that the statute violated Or. Const. art. I, § 8 because it was overbroad. The court observed that none of the allegations against defendant indicated that he made a campaign contribution. The court found that the facts he relied upon to support his demurrer did not appear on the face of the indictment. However, defendant was still entitled to assert his constitutional challenge by demurring to the indictment. The court found that Or. Rev. Stat. § 162.051 did not criminalize unreported campaign contributions. The court held that § 162.051 exempted any campaign contribution that was required to be reported by the donee under Or. Rev. Stat. § 260, even if the donee failed to report it. However, the state could prosecute defendant for bribery if it could prove that he did not intend the pecuniary benefit he gave to a candidate as a campaign contribution. Because the statute did not proscribe that conduct, it did not unconstitutionally burden defendant's rights under Or. Const. art. I, § 8. The trial court erred in granting defendant's demurrer.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the trial court's order that granted defendant's demurrer and dismissed three counts of bribery against him. The court remanded the cause for further proceedings.

State v. Bartley


CA No. A69895


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


121 Ore. App. 301; 854 P.2d 996; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 1046


November 9, 1992, Argued and submitted  
June 23, 1993, Decided  
June 23, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied September 29, Petition for Review Denied October 26, 1993 (318 Or 25).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Wheeler County. John V. Kelly, Judge. No. 323-C-90.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of his convictions in the Circuit Court, Wheeler County (Oregon) for assault in the fourth degree, rape in the first degree with a firearm, and two counts of burglary in the first degree with a firearm.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's adult stepdaughter claimed that defendant held a gun to her and raped her. The police found the gun in the stepdaughter's house and arrested defendant. Defendant claimed that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress the gun and motion to exclude evidence of his sexual relationship with his stepdaughter when she was a child. The court affirmed defendant's convictions. The court also held that the stepdaughter's testimony regarding her sexual relationship with defendant was relevant to the issue of consent, that the testimony helped explain why the stepdaughter acted the way she did, that the trial court did not abuse its discretion in allowing the evidence, and that the trial court gave a limiting instruction to the jury.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's convictions for assault in the fourth degree, rape in the first degree with a firearm, and two counts of burglary in the first degree with a firearm.

State v. Bodell


CA No. A72272


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


120 Ore. App. 548; 853 P.2d 841; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 972


August 12, 1992, Argued and submitted  
June 2, 1993, Decided  
June 2, 1993, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Tillamook County. David W. Hantke, Judge pro tempore. No. M-553.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a person alleged to be mentally ill, challenged an involuntary commitment order of the Circuit Court, Tillamook County (Oregon), under ORS 426.130. He argued that the trial court erred in finding that he was mentally ill under ORS 426.005(2) and in prohibiting him from purchasing or possessing a firearm under ORS 426.130(1)(b)(D).


OVERVIEW: A mental health examiner diagnosed appellant as suffering from paranoid schizophrenia. Appellant believed that he was the Lord and had been chosen to rule the world. As the result of his delusions, appellant threatened serious bodily harm to his child, his pregnant wife, and her unborn child. His wife feared for her own safety and that of her children. A psychiatrist who examined appellant testified that there was an actual danger that he would act on his delusional beliefs. On the instant appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err in concluding that appellant was dangerous to others and was mentally ill. The court disagreed with appellant's assertion that for purposes of ORS 426.130(1)(b)(D) there was no evidence of actual violence or a past pattern of threats involving unlawful violence. It found a pattern of behavior involving threats of unlawful violence that resulted from appellant's delusional ideation. Because it was raised for the first time on appeal, the court declined to address appellant's argument that ORS 426.130(1)(b)(D) was unconstitutional as applied because it restricted his right to bear arms.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed.

State v. Swartzendruber


CA No. A71550


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


120 Ore. App. 552; 853 P.2d 842; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 973


September 4, 1992, Argued and submitted  
June 2, 1993, Decided  
June 2, 1993, FILED


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from District Court, Benton County. Robert S. Gardner, Judge pro tempore. No. GA91-0019.



DISPOSITION: Conviction affirmed; remanded for resentencing.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a decision from the District Court, Benton County (Oregon), which convicted defendant of knowingly setting sight bait within 15 feet of a leghold trap for carnivores, a class A misdemeanor. Defendant objected to the presentation by the State at the sentencing hearing of illegally obtained evidence that had been suppressed by another trial court in three cases.


OVERVIEW: Defendant objected to the illegally obtained evidence, arguing that use of the evidence was unconstitutional, because it had been seized in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 9. The trial court allowed the State to present the evidence. The primary issue before the court was whether evidence seized in violation of Or. Const. art. I, § 9 could be considered in a sentencing hearing. The evidence had been previously suppressed on the ground that the officer had engaged in a "warrantless illegal entry." The court found that if the illegally seized evidence was presented at the sentencing hearing, the length of incarceration or probation might be determined or increased as a result of evidence seized in violation of constitutional rights. The court concluded that the vindication of defendant's rights under Or. Const. art. I, § 9 required that the illegally seized evidence be suppressed at sentencing. The court held that the imposition of jail time as a condition of probation for misdemeanors committed after November 1, 1989, but before June 10, 1991, violated the prohibition against ex post facto laws. Thus, the trial court erred in ordering defendant to serve jail time.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction of knowingly setting sight bait within 15 feet of a leghold trap for carnivores. The court remanded for resentencing.

889 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


GTE Northwest Inc. v. Public Util. Comm'n


CA No. A71751


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


120 Ore. App. 401; 852 P.2d 918; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 748


October 9, 1992, Argued and submitted  
May 19, 1993, Decided  
May 19, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Judicial Review of Administrative Rule. No. 88-954.



DISPOSITION: Rule held valid.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioners, two telecommunications public utilities that were regulated by respondent Public Utility Commission of Oregon (PUC), brought this action to challenge portions of the PUC rule codified at Or. Admin. R. 860-27-052, which governed the accounting treatment for affiliate transactions. It required that the utility's purchases be recorded at the lower of cost or market price while sales be recorded at the greater of cost or market price.


OVERVIEW: The rule was intended to prevent "cross-subsidization," by which intracorporate transfers were artificially inflated or deflated and could thereby be reflected in the rate structure at figures that did not accurately reflect the cost to or gain by the utilities or the affiliates. The court upheld the rule. The rule was consistent with the PUC's general rulemaking authority under Or. Rev. Stat. § 759.120. It governed the accounting treatment given to transactions between "regulated and unregulated accounts" as they affected the cost and gain profile of the regulated activity. The rule did not cause cross-subsidies, in violation of Or. Rev. Stat. § 759.030(5), by requiring either the regulated or unregulated account to absorb the shock of accounting entries that were not based on actual dollars spent or received. In the intracorporate transfer context, neither party actually gained or lost because the two parties were one. The rule did not give rise to a taking of shareholder property. That argument failed because it presupposed that the utilities were entitled to a return that was measured against something other than real investments, expenses, and profits.


OUTCOME: The court held that the rule was valid.

State v. Gonzalez


CA No. A69492


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


120 Ore. App. 249; 852 P.2d 851; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 787


June 8, 1992, Argued and submitted; December 2, 1992, Resubmitted In Banc  
May 19, 1993, Decided  
May 19, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied August 18, Petition for Review Denied November 16, 1993 (318 Or 61).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Clackamas County. Robert J. Morgan, Judge. No. 90-1448.



DISPOSITION: Reversed on appeal; affirmed on cross-appeal.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state challenged a pretrial order from the Circuit Court, Clackamas County (Oregon), which excluded evidence about defendant's blood alcohol content and his appearance when admitted to a hospital in his prosecution for manslaughter in the second degree, driving under the influence of intoxicants, and reckless driving. Defendant filed a cross-appeal from an order denying his motion to exclude certain hospital records.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was involved in a motor vehicle accident that resulted in a death. Defendant was injured in the accident. When he was treated at the hospital a blood-alcohol test was performed. The hospital records were admitted before the grand jury. Defendant was charged with manslaughter in the second degree, driving under the influence of intoxicants, and reckless driving. The trial court admitted the hospital records, but because the state violated Or. Rev. Stat. § 132.580(1) it barred evidence about defendant's blood alcohol content and testimony from an examining physician as to his state of intoxication in the emergency room after the accident. On appeal, the court affirmed the admission of the hospital records, but otherwise reversed. Section 132.580(1) only required that the names of witnesses who were examined before the grand jury and persons whose reports were submitted under § 132.320(2) be endorsed on the indictment. The toxicologist and the examining physician did not appear and testify before the grand jury. The hospital records were not reports under the statute. There was no violation of § 132.580 and the sanction of excluding the evidence was improperly applied.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the order that excluded evidence of defendant's blood alcohol content and his appearance when admitted to the hospital. The court affirmed the denial of defendant's motion to exclude the hospital records.

PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court, Benton County (Oregon) convicted defendant of possession of a controlled substance in violation of Or. Rev. Meltebeke v. Bureau of Labor & Indus.


CA No. A68770


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


120 Ore. App. 273; 852 P.2d 859; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 797; 63 Fair Empl. Prac. Cas. (BNA) 709; 63 Empl. Prac. Dec. (CCH) P42,638


September 21, 1992, Argued and submitted  
May 19, 1993, Decided  
May 19, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied September 29, Petition for Review Pending 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Judicial Review from Bureau of Labor and Industries. No. 29-90.



DISPOSITION: Reversed and remanded for reconsideration.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employer sought review after respondent Bureau of Labor and Industries (board) concluded that he had committed an unlawful practice by discriminating against one of his employees on the basis of religion.


OVERVIEW: The employer was a sole proprietor of a painting business. As an evangelical Christian, he believed that he had a duty to tell others, especially nonbelievers, about God and sinful conduct. That included informing others that, on the basis of elements of their lifestyles, they were sinners. The employer's "witnessing" was persistent because he believed that no one would ever be persuaded if he quit witnessing whenever a person lacked interest. After the employer discharged him for poor work performance, an employee filed an unlawful employment practice complaint with the board. The board concluded that the employer's religious advances constituted religious harassment. The court held that the decision deprived the employer of his rights under Or. Const. art. I, §§ 2 and 3. Protecting the sensibilities of a "reasonable person," without more, was not the least restrictive means available to advance the government's interest when religious freedom was at stake.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded for reconsideration the order that concluded that the employer committed an unlawful employment practice by "witnessing" to an employee.

 

893 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


City of Eugene v. Miller


CA Nos. A75143 (Control), A75145 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


119 Ore. App. 293; 851 P.2d 1142; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 590


December 21, 1992, Argued and submitted; February 10, 1993, Resubmitted In Banc  
April 21, 1993, Decided  
April 21, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied July 21, 1993. Petition for Review Allowed August 24, 1993 (317 Or 485).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from District Court, Lane County. Winfrid K. Liepe, Judge. Nos. 91-50062, 91-50073.



DISPOSITION: Reversed on both appeals.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from two convictions in the District Court, Lane County (Oregon) for violating a city code by selling joke books on a city sidewalk.


OVERVIEW: The disputed ordinance established a license requiring and licensing procedures for sidewalk vending, but licensed could only be issued for the sale of food, beverages, flowers, or balloons. Defendant did not have a license, nor had he applied for one. He claimed that the ordinance violated the free speech provisions of the state and federal constitutions. The court agreed, finding that the fact that the ordinance allowed some sales, while prohibiting defendant's sales, demonstrated that it reached his expression, without applying the same prohibition to other sales that were equally offensive to the city's regulatory objective of assuring that the streets and sidewalks were available for their principal purposes. The critical fact was not that the ordinance allowed some sales, but that it did not allow the sale of defendant's books. The ordinance did not proscribe or focus on an effect, but it proscribed the sale of particular commodities, including joke books, but allowed the sale of others. It regulated different exercises of the same commercial and communicative activity differently, on the basis of what was sold and communicated.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions.

State v. Perez


CA Nos. A72295 (Control), A72349, A72350 (Cases Consolidated)


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


119 Ore. App. 436; 851 P.2d 617; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 616


February 26, 1993, Argued and submitted  
April 21, 1993, Decided  
April 21, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied June 16, 1993. Petition for Review Denied July 27, 1993 (317 Or 272).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Klamath County. Donald A. W. Piper, Judge. Nos. 91-02100CR, 91-01159CR, 91-02067CR.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment from the Circuit Court, Klamath County (Oregon), which convicted and sentenced defendant for delivery of a controlled substance (DCS) and possession of a controlled substance (PCS), pursuant to Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.992. The trial court also overruled defendant's demurrer to his indictment, which had argued that the charged offenses were barred by state and federal constitutional ex post facto provisions.


OVERVIEW: Defendant alleged that until the legislature enacted a new sentencing scheme for drug offenses, there was no constitutionally valid category for his crimes. As a result, defendant argued that the effect of the new sentencing scheme was to create those crimes for the first time. Therefore, as applied to him, the new sentencing scheme was an ex post facto provision, because it made his acts greater crimes than when committed and increased the quantum of punishment. On appeal, however, the court upheld defendant's sentence and conviction. Specifically, the court determined that the new sentencing scheme codified at Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.996 did not violate the ex post facto clauses of Or. Const. art. I, § 21 or the federal constitution. Rather, the court held that the elements of the crimes of DCS and PCS under Or. Rev. Stat. § 475.992 were the same and that the new legislation controlled only the sentencing factors that Or. Rev. Stat. § 135.711 required the indictment to allege. Accordingly, the court concluded that the trial court properly overruled defendant's demurrer since the new legislation provided defendant with more protection.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment convicting and sentencing defendant for the delivery of a controlled substance (DCS) and possession of a controlled substance (PCS).

 

895 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


Benson v. City of Portland


CA No. A68717


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


119 Ore. App. 406; 850 P.2d 416; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 619


May 1, 1992, Argued and submitted  
April 21, 1993, Decided  
April 21, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Reconsideration Denied September 1, 1993. Petition for Review Denied October 26, 1993 (318 Or   ).


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Stephen B. Herrell, Judge. No. 9005-03217.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner, an owner of several unoccupied residential buildings, appealed from the judgment of the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon) upholding the orders of defendant city that the buildings were derelict and required to be registered pursuant to Portland, Or., City Code ch. 24.80. The owner had sought reversal of the city's orders on the grounds that ch. 24.80 violated the Due Process, Equal Protection, and Takings Clauses.


OVERVIEW: The owner of several unoccupied residential buildings that the city had declared derelict and required to be registered pursuant to ch. 24.80 brought the instant writ of review proceeding, and sought reversal of the city's orders on the grounds that ch. 24.80 violated the constitutional Due Process, Equal Protection, and Takings Clauses. The trial court upheld the orders, and the court affirmed. There was no taking because the city was not requiring him, as he assumed in his complaint, to have the buildings reoccupied. Consequently, his challenge was a facial one, and his burden was to show that ch. 24.80, first, necessarily referred to rentals or other undesired occupation by third parties and, second, compelled the owner either to rent the buildings or demolish them. He did not succeed on either prong. The owner mistakenly identified an uncompelled potential consequence of a regulation as being part of the regulation. The city simply did not require the owner to lease his property to others, and he advanced no other basis for concluding that his property had been taken within the meaning of either constitution. The owner's due process and equal protection also lacked merit.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's upholding the city's orders for the owner to register his unoccupied buildings as derelict.

State v. Salzmann


CA No. A75561


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


119 Ore. App. 217; 850 P.2d 1122; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 566



April 14, 1993, Decided  
April 14, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] Petition for Review Denied June 22, 1993 (317 Or 163).


PRIOR HISTORY: On appellant's petition for reconsideration of an order dated October 30, 1992, dismissing the appeal; Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Kimberly C. Frankel, Judge. No. C9111-36157.



DISPOSITION: Reconsideration allowed; appeal dismissed; order modified to delete costs.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant filed a petition for review, which the court treated as one for reconsideration, pursuant to Or. R. App. P. 9.15(1), of an earlier decision dismissing defendant's appeal, pursuant to Or. R. App. P. 7.15(1), on the ground of former jeopardy stemming from a mistrial on charges for rape, kidnapping, and robbery.


OVERVIEW: During defendant's trial, defendant moved for a mistrial on several occasions claiming that the prosecutors had engaged in misconduct. After the trial court denied the motions, the jury was unable to reach a verdict and a mistrial was declared. Therefore, defendant unsuccessfully moved to dismiss the charges on the ground of former jeopardy. On reconsideration of its earlier ruling dismissing defendant's appeal, the court modified its previous order holding that defendant did not have a right to a direct interlocutory appeal after the trial court denied the motion to dismiss the charges, and modified the previous order to delete costs. In pertinent part, the court determined that defendant's argument, that protections of the double jeopardy clause of Or. Const. art. I, § 12, became meaningless unless a defendant had a right to an interlocutory appeal, had no merit because the right to appeal in Oregon was a statutory right and not a constitutional right. Similarly, the court held that the equal protection clause of Or. Const. art. I, § 20, was not violated because the remedy of a direct interlocutory appeal was equally unavailable to all defendants.


OUTCOME: The court allowed reconsideration of its earlier decision, modified its previous order dismissing defendant's appeal, and modified the previous order to delete costs.

899 of 1650 DOCUMENTS


State v. Reichsfeld


CA No. A71206


COURT OF APPEALS OF OREGON


118 Ore. App. 592; 848 P.2d 639; 1993 Ore. App. LEXIS 438


August 28, 1992, Argued and submitted  
March 17, 1993, Decided  
March 17, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court, Multnomah County. Charles B. Guinasso, Judge pro tempore. No. 9101 30435.



DISPOSITION: Affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant challenged a judgment by the Circuit Court, Multnomah County (Oregon), which convicted him of criminal possession of a forgery device under ORS 165.032(1)(b). Defendant argued that the statute was unconstitutionally vague.


OVERVIEW: Airport security personnel found large cardboard placards that were oversized replicas of Oregon, Utah, and New Jersey driver's licenses in defendant's bags. The placards had a cutout in the upper corner. Such placards were commonly used to forge driver's licenses. Defendant was convicted of criminal possession of a forgery device under ORS 165.032(1)(b). On appeal, defendant argued that the statute was unconstitutionally vague because its inclusion of the words "capable of" forgery were unclear. The court disagreed and affirmed defendant's conviction. The court held that defendant's conduct was squarely within the statute's prohibition. The court held that the key to a vagueness challenge was the statute's failure to provide a defendant with fair notice of the prohibited conduct. In this case, the court held that any person of ordinary intelligence would have no difficulty knowing that defendant's possession of the placards, if performed with the requisite intent, fell within the ambit of the statutory prohibition.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment.
